    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }

      AppleID.auth.init({

This is the story of how she got that way.<br /><br />Frankie Landau-Banks. No longer the kind of girl to take "no" for an answer. Especially when "no" means she's excluded from her boyfriend's all-male secret society. Not when her ex-boyfriend shows up in the strangest of places. Not when she knows she's smarter than any of them. When she knows Matthew's lying to her. And when there are so many, many pranks to be done.</span>

<div id="review_169819678" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/169819678" />
    <a title="Emily May" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4622890-emily-may"><img alt="Emily May" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1528390751p2/4622890.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/169819678?book_show_action=true">May 20, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Emily May" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Emily May" href="/user/show/4622890-emily-may">Emily May</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780786838196" href="/book/show/6663773-the-disreputable-history-of-frankie-landau-banks">review of another edition</a>


          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Emily May by: </span>
      <a href="/user/show/2250020-tatiana">Tatiana</a>
  </div>


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4622890-emily-may?shelf=young-adult">young-adult</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4622890-emily-may?shelf=2011">2011</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer169819678" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6088010296115562772">ooh... secret societies and gender politics...<br/><br/>If you're female and between the ages of about 12 and 25, I cannot think of a single reason why you shouldn't read this book. It's fantastic. Both highly political and incredibly funny - it's the book I wish I'd been given to see me through being a teenager and to prepare me for later life. And no, I never went to an elite prep school with a bunch of stuffy trainee 'old boys' and a 60 year old all-male secret society... but I, like every girl I know,</span>
  <span id="freeText6088010296115562772" style="display:none">ooh... secret societies and gender politics...<br /><br />If you're female and between the ages of about 12 and 25, I cannot think of a single reason why you shouldn't read this book. It's fantastic. Both highly political and incredibly funny - it's the book I wish I'd been given to see me through being a teenager and to prepare me for later life. And no, I never went to an elite prep school with a bunch of stuffy trainee 'old boys' and a 60 year old all-male secret society... but I, like every girl I know, could have done with the reassurance that being your own person is more important than fitting the mold and that women are worth more than just a chest measurement. The story spoke to me on many levels and addressed issues that I have written articles on and feel very strongly about.<br /><br />On the surface, it's a high school tale of cliques, first loves and mischief - quite like the author's <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/301022.The_Boyfriend_List_15_Guys__11_Shrink_Appointments__4_Ceramic_Frogs_and_Me__Ruby_Oliver__Ruby_Oliver___1_" title="The Boyfriend List 15 Guys, 11 Shrink Appointments, 4 Ceramic Frogs and Me, Ruby Oliver (Ruby Oliver, #1) by E. Lockhart" rel="nofollow noopener">The Boyfriend List: 15 Guys, 11 Shrink Appointments, 4 Ceramic Frogs and Me, Ruby Oliver</a> and the other Ruby Oliver books. I enjoyed reading about Ruby and frequently related to her but, for me, Frankie Landau-Banks was all the more kick-ass, funny and just so memorable. She's a 5-star heroine and the perfect partner in crime... if only she were real. <br /><br />The writing in this novel was flawless with some hilarious dialogue between the characters, particularly regarding some of Frankie's ridiculous neglected positives, it's such a silly idea that shouldn't be so funny but I have no idea how many times I must have laughed. Little scenes like this are what made the book for me:<br /><br /><i>"They're not puppets, they're muppets," said Frankie. "I have a serious and justified love for Kermit that I will parage to the end."<br />"Parage?"<br />"Parage. The neglected postive of disparage."<br />"You mean defend. You will defend Kermit to the end."<br />"Parage."<br />"Praise?"<br />"Parage. I will parage him. And Animal, too. I love Animal. I used to watch that show on DVD all the time when I was little."<br />Trish changed the subject. "We should do facials and paint our toenails Friday before they pick us up. What do you say, blow through dinner and come back here for girlie stuff?"<br />Frankie said, "You're on. When we're finished, we'll be absolutely sheveled."<br />"You'll be sheveled," said Trish. "I'm a normal person."</i><br /><br />I mean, come on, that's funny. And she's so effin' stubborn it's great. I just loved Frankie and loved the plot and loved the book. <b>I took notes on the damn thing</b>. No, really, there are parts of this book that you just have to note down. By 'you', of course, I actually mean me.<br /><br />I also want to point out for all you cynical people who "bah humbug" at novels set in high schools following a girl through her relationships and pranky misdeeds... this really is a great political statement. But it's in the dialogue and Frankie's awesomeness that it's revealed, sometimes subtle and sometimes not. My favourite thing about it is how the school represents today's society as a whole and the truths about the equality myth. Because, sadly, even though men and women are supposed to have the same opportunities and they are now allowed into the same professions, they sit at the same tables and they even become friends, beneath it all there is still an inner circle - rather like a secret society - that continues to slam the door in a woman's face. But better than this metaphor is the message behind it: that if you put your mind to it, you don't have to accept the way things are. That you have the ability to change the way of the world. Or the way of a prep school. Like Frankie does.<br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="6088010296115562772" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1385195635?book_show_action=true">Sep 06, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Ariel" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Ariel" href="/user/show/6536286-ariel">Ariel</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781471404405" href="/book/show/22907596-the-disreputable-history-of-frankie-landau-banks">review of another edition</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1385195635" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17111198583440646231">A solid, teen, feminist novel!<br/><br/>I really enjoy e. lockhart's writing and this book didn't let me down! A story about a girl navigating a boarding school where boys have some /special/ privileges over girls, she starts to fight against that system. I liked the main character, enjoyed the boarding school setting, and really appreciated the feminist angle. My only reason for not giving this 5 stars is that I don't think that a) it did anything particularly new with plot or character development, and </span>
  <span id="freeText17111198583440646231" style="display:none">A solid, teen, feminist novel!<br /><br />I really enjoy e. lockhart's writing and this book didn't let me down! A story about a girl navigating a boarding school where boys have some /special/ privileges over girls, she starts to fight against that system. I liked the main character, enjoyed the boarding school setting, and really appreciated the feminist angle. My only reason for not giving this 5 stars is that I don't think that a) it did anything particularly new with plot or character development, and b) it very firmly felt like a simple introduction to feminism - the lessons here are important, but are also very rudimentary and simple.<br /><br />Definitely recommend this to any teenagers who like boarding school books and who want to learn some basics of feminism!</span>
  <a data-text-id="17111198583440646231" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_269237710" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/269237710" />
    <a title="Nataliya" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3672777-nataliya"><img alt="Nataliya" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1605995014p2/3672777.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/269237710?book_show_action=true">Jan 28, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Nataliya" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Nataliya" href="/user/show/3672777-nataliya">Nataliya</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3672777-nataliya?shelf=awesome-kickass-heroines">awesome-kickass-heroines</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3672777-nataliya?shelf=for-my-future-hypothetical-daughter">for-my-future-hypothetical-daughter</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3672777-nataliya?shelf=2014-reads">2014-reads</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3672777-nataliya?shelf=2012-reads">2012-reads</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer269237710" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3695353014019616236">You need to look past the surface cuteness to see this story for what it really is.<br/><br/>- It's about power. The allure of it, the desire for it, the sharp understanding of it.<br/>- It's about ambition, unashamed and unabashed.<br/>- It's about forcing your way through the doors that are not meant to open for you.<br/><br/><b>It's about the formation of a future astute politician.</b><br/><br/>Meet Frankie Landau-Banks.<blockquote>
  <i>“And so, another possibility—the possibility I hold out for—is that Frankie Landau-Banks will open the doors she is </i></blockquote></span>
  <span id="freeText3695353014019616236" style="display:none">You need to look past the surface cuteness to see this story for what it really is.<br><br>- It's about power. The allure of it, the desire for it, the sharp understanding of it.<br>- It's about ambition, unashamed and unabashed.<br>- It's about forcing your way through the doors that are not meant to open for you.<br><br><b>It's about the formation of a future astute politician.</b><br><br>Meet Frankie Landau-Banks.<blockquote>
  <i>“And so, another possibility—the possibility I hold out for—is that Frankie Landau-Banks will open the doors she is trying to get through.<br>And she will grow up to change the world.”</i>
</blockquote>E. Lockhart is a mastermind who knows very well how to play with her readers' perceptions and expectations. In writing a story about a young woman trying so hard to grow out of being a 'Bunny Rabbit', a 'harmless' 'pretty package' sweet and sensitive young girl supportively watching her boyfriend and his friends learn to grow into their birthright of securely powerful WASPs, she created a story that can be so temping to pigeonhole:<br><br>- into the story about a boarding school and secret societies.<br>- into the story of learning about feminism.<br>- into an unfortunate love story.<br>- into a coming of age story.<br>- into a story about the silly problems of those who have all the privilege and none of the real struggle.<br><br>But it's more that any of the above.<br><br>And I stick with my idea that <b>the glue that keeps this story together and elevates it head and shoulders above so many others is precisely the theme of power and ambition and politics</b>; all this in a young woman who typically is not associated with any of the above, whose role is traditionally supposed to be something different - so different, that when she deviates from it, she's viewed somewhere on the borderline between 'obsessed' and 'psychotic'. <br><br><b>But Frankie knows what she wants.</b><blockquote>
  <img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1402590072i/9957039.jpg" width="320" class="gr-hostedUserImg">
  <br>
  <br>
  <i>“Matthew had called her harmless. Harmless. And being with him made Frankie feel squashed into a box—a box where she was expected to be sweet and sensitive (but not oversensitive); a box for young and pretty girls who were not as bright or powerful as their boyfriends. A box for people who were not forces to be reckoned with.<br><br><b>Frankie wanted to be a force.</b>”</i>
</blockquote>Frankie Landau-Banks comes from a life of privilege. Her family is rich and connected. Her exclusive New England preparatory school is a feeder school for Harvard. Everyone is rich (but , of course, tastefully not flaunting it), mostly WASP, smart and well-spoken and bristling with security that befits those destined by birthright and money to make it big in this world, to rule. Forging connections here is important because Frankie's classmates will be the ruling elite. One of them may even run for President one day.<blockquote>
  <i>“Senior’s boyhood days were still the largest looming factor in his conception of himself. His former schoolfellows were his closest friends. They were the people he golfed with, the people he invited for drinks, the people whose country homes he visited on vacation. They were people he recommended for jobs; people who sent him patients and asked him to sit on boards of arts organizations; people who connected him to other people.”</i>
</blockquote><b>The problem is: while they can strive to be the President, in this world Frankie's brightest ambition *should* be the First Lady. </b>Support on the sidelines. A pretty package. Unable to enter the Old Boys world.<br><br>And that stings.<blockquote>
  <i>“And because of her sex, because of her age, because (perhaps) of her religion and her feminism, she could sit at their table every day and she would never, never, ever get in.<br><br>Frankie had fallen in love not only with Matthew but with his group of friends. And she knew they didn’t rate her as anyone important.”</i>
  <br>
  <br>
  <img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1402589873i/9957005.jpg" width="300" class="gr-hostedUserImg">
</blockquote>That's where this book drew criticisms from many: that Frankie wants to assert power by infiltrating the world of the secret boy society, that she does not instead go and start her own thing (like her Berkeley-educated sister suggests), that she does not find something worthier than the Old Boy power squabbles, that she wants to take over something that the 'old society' created instead of proudly denouncing it and building something new. <br><br>Maybe her idealism is simply lacking?<blockquote>
  <i>“Please, that is so antiquated. The institutions of male supremacy only have real power over you if you buy into that notion. Go found your own club and tell them they can’t join. Or better yet, drop the idea of clubs altogether because they’re exclusionary, and embrace some other, more flexible way of connecting with people.”<br><br>“But, Zada.” Frankie wanted to explain about the door being closed, about wanting to push through the door, about wanting not to feel small and second-best at the table. But Zada cut her off.”</i>
</blockquote><b>Or maybe Frankie already realizes at the age of fifteen, in the middle of her “intellectual explosion sophomore year”: the power is where it is,</b> and instead of carving herself a niche on the outskirts of the 'old boys' universe - a niche that the ones in power can go on blissfully ignoring - she does not see why exactly she cannot just join, participate - and, perhaps, overtake. <br><br>She wants in, and she wants her share of existing power. She refuses to be kept on the sidelines, and in order to avoid it you need to step into the WASP nest. You need to go off the beaten path of creating your own niche and claw your way into the rigid the world that already has power, and bend it into the shape that suits you - instead of waiting for someone else to finally hand you what you deserve.<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1402589873i/9957006._SX540_.jpg" width="400" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><blockquote>
  <i>“Frankie Landau-Banks is an off-roader.<br><br>She might, in fact, go crazy, as has happened to a lot of people who break rules. Not the people who play at rebellion but really only solidify their already dominant positions in society—as did Matthew and most of the other Bassets—but those who take some larger action that disrupts the social order. Who try to push through the doors that are usually closed to them. They do sometimes go crazy, these people, because the world is telling them not to want the things they want. It can seem saner to give up—but then one goes insane from giving up.”</i>
</blockquote>Frankie's plans may have not exactly gone as she expected. Yes, she was misunderstood. Yes, the ones wanting to pigeonhole her found ways to continue doing it instead of taking a good look at their perceptions and prejudices. Yes, the others may have thought the problem was with Frankie and not the rest of her insular world. Yes, she was hurt and lonely and angry.<br><br>But none of this made her doubt even for a moment that there are things that need to be changed, and things that she wants, and that she is not going to quit doing exactly what she wants to do and achieve what she wants to achieve.<blockquote>
  <i>“Frankie did not accept life as it was presently occurring. It was a fundamental element of her character. Life as it was presently occurring was not acceptable to her.”</i>
</blockquote>And it's for this tenacity, perseverance and refusal to neatly fit into the predetermined role in somebody else's universe that I love Frankie Landau-Banks.<br><br>Don't cross her path and try to hold her back.<br><b>Because the power is hers and she will do amazing things with it someday.</b><br>We can only hope those great things will also be good.<blockquote>
  <i>“It is better to be alone, she figures, than to be with someone who can’t see who you are. It is better to lead than to follow. It is better to speak up than stay silent. It is better to open doors than to shut them on people.<br><br>She will not be simple and sweet. She will not be what people tell her she should be. That Bunny Rabbit is dead.”</i>
</blockquote></span>
  <a data-text-id="3695353014019616236" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/45618-karen?shelf=why-yes-i-ya">why-yes-i-ya</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/45618-karen?shelf=and-so-this-is-grad-school">and-so-this-is-grad-school</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer122246128" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15099119559608442057">mary lou retton two times!!<br/><br/>this is the teen fiction the good girls read. girls without problems like anorexia or cutting or promiscuity or retrograde amnesia. the ones whose mothers don't need to worry about them rotting their brains on vampires and rainbow parties. the ones janis ian envied:<br/><br/>"high school girls with clear skinned smiles who married young and then retired"<br/><br/>i mean, it is published by disney, so i wasn't expecting smut and guts, but it's pretty precious and twee, qualities which usua</span>
  <span id="freeText15099119559608442057" style="display:none">mary lou retton two times!!<br><br>this is the teen fiction the good girls read. girls without problems like anorexia or cutting or promiscuity or retrograde amnesia. the ones whose mothers don't need to worry about them rotting their brains on vampires and rainbow parties. the ones janis ian envied:<br><br>"high school girls with clear skinned smiles who married young and then retired"<br><br>i mean, it is published by disney, so i wasn't expecting smut and guts, but it's pretty precious and twee, qualities which usually give me the angers.<br><br>reading the prologue, i thought i was going to hate it - all those cutesy perky wordplays - eventually you learn there is a reason for it, but that doesn't make it any less irritating.it is like the kid sister of <i>special topics in calamity physics</i>, a book i rather enjoyed, but i can totally understand other people's hatred of. <br><br>at the end of the day, it is a cutesy adventure story for <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/120774208" rel="nofollow noopener">spunky girls.</a> like... like... mary lou retton!<br><br>there are a number of positive things about this book; smart girl with energy and a mischievous mind who tries to overthrow an all-male secret society (who hasn't been there, right??) but for all her grrl power, she ain't no feminist. even when attempting flirtation with her older boy love interest, she mentally tests and rejects a number of responses, rating them on the basis of "how he will respond and what it will say about me as a person." what happened to just being yourself, and not a weirdly manipulative and calculating freak? where is <i>sassy</i> magazine c. 1991 for this chick??<br><br>and at the end <b>THIS IS WHERE I AM TALKING A LITTLE ABOUT THE ENDING OF THE BOOK, DIG??</b> when she fails to win the respect of the boys she has been following around and on the one hand being a secret leader to, but on the other hand, really just trying to impress them so they will accept her regardless of her gender, she gets a little shrieky and petulant which just makes her come across as a victim, which is way unattractive. plus, the club she wants to get into is super-lame. a real girl-power book would have her start her own secret society with the couple of girls who are worth anything in this book and like band together in a prank-off (i mean, isn't this practically a disney template?? stop slacking, mickey...) and then it would all end in a spectacular floor routine in a leotard the color of victory.<br><br>it's not painful to read or anything, but i have a feeling elizabeth would fulking HATE it...<br><br><img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1528725630i/25720315.png" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><a href="http://bloggycomelately.com/" rel="nofollow noopener">come to my blog!</a></span>

<div id="review_171864903" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/171864903" />
    <a title="Wendy Darling" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2745288-wendy-darling"><img alt="Wendy Darling" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1379959930p2/2745288.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/171864903?book_show_action=true">May 28, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Wendy Darling" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Wendy Darling" href="/user/show/2745288-wendy-darling">Wendy Darling</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2745288-wendy-darling?shelf=brain-candy">brain-candy</a>,

              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2745288-wendy-darling?shelf=read-2012">read-2012</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2745288-wendy-darling?shelf=heroines-wimpy">heroines-wimpy</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2745288-wendy-darling?shelf=huge-disappointments">huge-disappointments</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2745288-wendy-darling?shelf=young-adult">young-adult</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2745288-wendy-darling?shelf=realistic-fiction">realistic-fiction</a>,

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer171864903" class="readable"

<span id="freeTextContainer8171793305416325864">I've pushed through to 100 pages, but I just can't go on. For all its braininess, this book feels very juvenile to me, except that girls in most middle grade books are rarely this boy-crazy--and few of them have so little else going on their lives.<br/><br/>Here are some things that make it feel very young to me:<br/><br/>--the story seems to be centered around a "caper" in which Frankie tries to infiltrate a secret boys club<br/>--three boys who don't recognize Frankie after her body develops (or pretend not to, anyway</span>
  <span id="freeText8171793305416325864" style="display:none">I've pushed through to 100 pages, but I just can't go on. For all its braininess, this book feels very juvenile to me, except that girls in most middle grade books are rarely this boy-crazy--and few of them have so little else going on their lives.<br /><br />Here are some things that make it feel very young to me:<br /><br />--the story seems to be centered around a "caper" in which Frankie tries to infiltrate a secret boys club<br />--three boys who don't recognize Frankie after her body develops (or pretend not to, anyway)<br />--a full page about boogers<br />--occasional overuse of exclamation points<br />--and above all else, the writing style<br /><br />Written in the third person with an occasional interruption by some unknown narrator, it's all done in arch prose that strives very hard to be humorous and clever without being very smart. Or deep. Or interesting. At least to me, because this book certainly has its share of fans.<br /><br />I'll leave you with just one paragraph so you can decide whether this writing floats your boat and if you want to read any further:<br /><br /><i>Most young women, when confronted with the peculiarly male nature of certain social events--usually those incorporating beer or other substances guaranteed to kill off brain cells, and often involving either the freezing-cold outdoors or the near-suffocating heat of a filthy dorm room, but which can also, in more intellectual circles, include the watching of boring Russian films--will react in one of three ways.</i><br /><br />Honestly, the whole premise of this felt like a brain candy book disguised as something much more smart or meaningful. Boys aren't paying attention to you? Who cares? Not me.<br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="8171793305416325864" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_53721652" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/53721652" />
    <a title="Tatiana" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2250020-tatiana"><img alt="Tatiana" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1249783659p2/2250020.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/53721652?book_show_action=true">Apr 23, 2009</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Tatiana" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Tatiana" href="/user/show/2250020-tatiana">Tatiana</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Audio CD 9781423366812" href="/book/show/6774949-the-disreputable-history-of-frankie-landau-banks">review of another edition</a>

          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">everyone </span></div>

        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2250020-tatiana?shelf=2010">2010</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2250020-tatiana?shelf=i-am-so-over-you">i-am-so-over-you</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2250020-tatiana?shelf=ya">ya</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2250020-tatiana?shelf=national-book-award">national-book-award</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2250020-tatiana?shelf=2018">2018</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2250020-tatiana?shelf=book-club">book-club</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2250020-tatiana?shelf=2009">2009</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2250020-tatiana?shelf=printz">printz</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2250020-tatiana?shelf=2016">2016</a>
          </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer53721652" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15076625403814229495"><b>Update 8/21/2018</b><br/>What a difference 2 years make. It feels like this book has aged 30 years since I read it last time in 2016. Amazing how much the conversation around feminism and privilege changed in such a short time. Hard to recommend this book now though. <br/><br/><b>Update 9/16/2016</b><br/>Considering it's a 8-year old YA novel and how much YA has improved over these 8 years, I think it held up pretty well. Maybe not 5 stars any more, but still worthy of a read.<br/><br/><b>Originally</b><br/>This is definitely one of those clever a</span>
  <span id="freeText15076625403814229495" style="display:none"><b>Update 8/21/2018</b><br />What a difference 2 years make. It feels like this book has aged 30 years since I read it last time in 2016. Amazing how much the conversation around feminism and privilege changed in such a short time. Hard to recommend this book now though. <br /><br /><b>Update 9/16/2016</b><br />Considering it's a 8-year old YA novel and how much YA has improved over these 8 years, I think it held up pretty well. Maybe not 5 stars any more, but still worthy of a read.<br /><br /><b>Originally</b><br />This is definitely one of those clever and sophisticated YA books that is a must-read for any teenage girl and adults as well. It is not limited to some teen romance and angst, but it is a commentary on social and gender dynamics.<br /><br />Frankie might be overly ambitious and driven, and she might not have made all the smartest decisions, trying to get appreciation from people who by default perceive her to be no more than a pretty pet, but she figured out the main thing right: it is important to be your own person (not just a man's arm-candy), to be appreciated for your personal qualities and talents and not only for your adorable face and nice figure. <br /><br />Writing style is flawless, dialog superb. Now, having read this novel second time, I am upgrading it to 5 stars. "The Disreputable History" is definitely E. Lockhart's best book to date and the one I expect to re-read over and over again.</span>
  <a data-text-id="15076625403814229495" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

      <span class='likeItContainer' id='like_container_review_53721652'><a id="like_count_review_53721652" rel="nofollow" href="/rating/voters/53721652?resource_type=Review"><span class="likesCount">69 likes</span></a>&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;<a rel="nofollow" class="jsLike like_it gr-button gr-button--small" href="https://www.goodreads.com/user/new?remember=true&amp;return_url=%2Fbook%2Fshow%2F1629601.The_Disreputable_History_of_Frankie_Landau_Banks">Like</a></span>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_100197354" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/100197354" />
    <a title="CeJayCe" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3304158-cejayce"><img alt="CeJayCe" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1461049154p2/3304158.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/100197354?book_show_action=true">Apr 26, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="CeJayCe" class="user" itemprop="url" name="CeJayCe" href="/user/show/3304158-cejayce">CeJayCe</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="did not like it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">did not like it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer100197354" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4817908887381943184">I'm more than a third of the way into it and, I've gotta say, I'm disappointed. There's too much build up. So far, all I'm getting is a over-analyzation of every aspect of Frankie's relationship with Matthew. You'd think this far in I'd at least get some kind of clue as to what she's done that was so big. <br/><br/>Now I've gotten to Porter and the cheese fries and, even though she may be making a valid point--Porter shouldn't be more worried about her well-being now just because she's prettier, she sound</span>
  <span id="freeText4817908887381943184" style="display:none">I'm more than a third of the way into it and, I've gotta say, I'm disappointed. There's too much build up. So far, all I'm getting is a over-analyzation of every aspect of Frankie's relationship with Matthew. You'd think this far in I'd at least get some kind of clue as to what she's done that was so big. <br /><br />Now I've gotten to Porter and the cheese fries and, even though she may be making a valid point--Porter shouldn't be more worried about her well-being now just because she's prettier, she sounds like a crazy bitch the way she carries on. And, not that it's right, but what Porter's saying is true. I mean, it's not like it's not true that guys typically go after girls with prettier faces and bigger boobs. There's no point in pretending we don't know that. So Frankie may be a bit more on the radar than she was last year. Still, it was awful presumptuous of Porter to tell her that. It's like saying, "I know I didn't tell you to watch out for other guys when we were dating, but you're kinda hot now so I thought I should let you know."<br /><br />While I commend Frankie for calling him on it, I can't say I agree with the way she handled it. (i.e. Screaming and raising hell in a public place.) And then she goes on about being "proud" of herself.<br /><br /><blockquote>Frankie hadn't <i>liked</i> herself while she'd been yelling at Porter--but she had admired herself.</blockquote><br /><br />How, exactly, can you feel both of those ways at once? About <i>yourself</i>?<br /><br />And then it goes on to say:<br /><br /><blockquote>So I was a monster, she thought. At least I wasn't someone's little sister, someone's girlfriend, some sophmore, some girl--someone whose opinions don't matter.</blockquote><br /><br />So you'd rather be a monster, Frankie? You'd rather people look at you and see a raging lunatic if you think it gets your point across? Because I can honestly say NO ONE is going to take you seriously or even listen to you for that matter when you act like you've just lost your mind. You can be composed and still get your point across. You say that the other ways girls deal with being offended are pouting, grumbling, moping, or whining (all of which YOU do yourself throughout the story). But you did not list "talking". Maybe it's because you haven't tried that yet, but when you actual state your opinion in a composed voice like a sensible person, people tend to listen. Especially when you can back up whatever you're saying. And since when do the opinions of sisters, girlfriends, sophmores, and girls in general not matter?<br /><br /><br />And if you ask me, Frankie's whole "I'm an independent woman" campaign never stops backfiring. She's really no better than all the spineless girls who let their boyfriends walk all over them. Only, instead of doing everything he wants, she's doing whatever he DOESN'T want her to do or doesn't expect her to do. Which really isn't what she wants to do at all. Like eating garlic knots, which she hates, just because Alpha assumed she wouldn't want any. Yeah, she ate them to prove him wrong. But what good is that when he was right anyway and she really DIDN'T want any? All she did was make herself eat something she hated. From what I could see, it didn't earn her anymore respect from him. So she's still letting her boyfriend (not to mention his friends) control all her actions.<br /><br />And is it me, or is it really hard to believe she really cares for him at all? I mean, it seems like she's so busy over-analyzing everything he says and does to really enjoy the relationship their in. <br /><br />Prime example:<br /><br /><blockquote>Frankie felt herself flush. "Thank you."<br />"You're welcome," said Matthew. "I like a girl who knows how to take a compliment. You know how so many girls are all, 'Oh, me? I'm not pretty. I'm a hag.'"<br />"Yeah."<br />"Well, it's so much nicer when someone just says thank you. So don't become that girl, okay?"<br />"That haggy girl? Okay."<br /><br />--<br /><br />A tiny part of her wanted to go over to him and shout, "I can feel like a hag some days if I want!"</blockquote><br /><br />...What the hell was that, Frankie? Again, she's trying so hard not to let him control her that she's making herself look stupid. He was complimenting her. And basically saying, "Don't change because I like you the way that you are." Now if he had told her to change she would've had a fit too. But instead he tells her not to change and she thinks crap like, "Oh, yeah? I can be a hag if I want to!" Yeah. Yeah, you can, Frankie. It's true that he can't tell you how to feel or act. But get real. No girl WANTS to be a hag. That was a pretty suckish rebuttal. (Are you really on the debate team?) And his comment didn't need an argument. Hardly anything he says or does really NEEDS an argument.<br /><br />And I've yet to see this so-called "genius" she possesses. I admit she seems more aware of herself and her relationship than most girls. But she doesn't ever DO anything about it. She admits that Matthew wants her in his world but doesn't really want anything to do with hers. He doesn't come to her dorm, attempt to be nice to Trish the way she does with his friends, or even ask about her family. And Frankie acknowledges all this and does nothing. She's OKAY with it.<br /><br /><blockquote>Lots of girls don't notice when they are in this situation. They are so focused on their boyfriends that they don't remember they had a life at all before their romances, so they don't become upset that the boyfriend isn't interested.<br /><br />Frankie did notice--but she wasn't sure she cared.</blockquote><br /><br />Why? Why wouldn't she care? She's so concerned with being her own person, but she wants to immerse herself in his world. Every compliment and gift he gives her needs to be analyzed for any double meanings, but he doesn't have to show any interest in her life, friends, or family. It's like the thing with her making up the stupid words and then him correcting her. I mean, if you care about someone you wouldn't want them walking around using words with no real meaning. They'd sound stupid. <i>She</i> sounds stupid when she does it. I would've corrected her too if I were him. But instead of just listening to the dictionary, she gets upset and oversensitive (yes, Frankie, you're oversensitive. Matthew AND Porter have told you this. They're not making it up.) and continues to use the word and, therefore, sound like an idiot.<br /><br />My point is, Frankie's not arguing on the points she should be arguing on. The book is trying to give us the impression that she's set so far apart from average high-school girls, but she's really not.<br /><br />Even her mentality is achingly typical at times:<br /><br /><blockquote>--but she was a heterosexual sophmore with no boyfriend and no social power (especially now that Zada had graduated). On what planet would a girl in her position refuse to go to a golf course party with Matthew Livingston?</blockquote><br /><br />Yes, he's the cool senior and you're the average sophmore nobody. I think you've stressed that enough Frankie. I was hoping for something more original.<br /><br />Futhermore, she's a stalker. And a pathetic one at that. She may claim she's following her boyfriend and all the dumb guys around because she's advocating some stupid right women supposedly have to join their club. But the real deal is that she just wants to be a part of that inner circle. She feels like they don't value her that much, which is true. And she's so worried about being friends with them and being popular that she's willing to follow them around in the dark and watch them while they drink beer, eat chips, and talk guy stuff. I believe her sister Zada said it best:<br /><br /><blockquote>"Please, that is so antiquated. The institution of male supremacy only have real power over you if you buy into that notion. Go found your own club and tell them <i>they</i> can't join. Or better yet, drop the idea of clubs altogether because they're exclusionary, and embrace some other, more flexible way of connecting with people."<br />"But, Zada." Frankie wanted to explain about the door being closed, about wanting to push through the door, about wanting not to feel small and second-best at the caf table. But Zada cut her off.<br />"Don't stress over this, Frankie. It's okay if Matthew's in some dumb drinking club that you're not in. Just let him be in it and go do your own thing."</blockquote><br /><br />Thank you, Zada. Clearly, Frankie doesn't give a crap, really, about her right as a women to be in that club. (Which she really doesn't have, anyway. It's not like it's supported by the school or anything. And why would she want to be in it? It's a group of guys bonding with other guys in a way that they can't bond with girls. Why does she have to interfere with that? There's absolutely nothing wrong with it.) She just wants to do whatever her boyfriend is doing so she'll feel accepted and not "second-best at the caf table".<br /><br />And all for a group of people whom she clearly cares about more than they care about her. (And who will be graduating next year and leaving her anyway.) And she can claim to be so into Matthew all she wants. It's obvious none of this is about love. This about her being whiny and bratty. Like a little kid sibling that wants to do everything and be everywhere their older sibling is. It's annoying and not so endearing after the age of six.<br /><br />Less on Frankie and more on the book in it's entirety, it didn't live up to my expectations at all. For one thing, there were a lot of characters and a lot of names that seemed like they needed remembering because they showed up so often (which they didn't, really) and there were hardly any descriptions. I couldn't imagine a face for anyone. Not even Frankie. In the very first pages it basically said she went from gawky to hot and that's it. And the book would sometimes throw out names of people that had never been mentioned like you were supposed to know who they were. No background, no description, just a name. <br /><br />The omniscient narrator was a bit difficult to follow sometimes too. Sometimes it seemed like they were making a point like, "Hey, this little tidbit is important. It's going to come up later, so remember it." And then other times they were just rambling, almost indistinguishable from Frankie's thoughts and what we were supposed to infer that she was thinking.<br /><br />This is without a doubt the longest review I've ever done. And I typically do pretty long reviews. But this here is like my book on what's wrong with the book. E. Lockhart really disappointed me with this. Her other books are so great. I was prepared to be completely dazzled and now...*sigh*. If I could give it my own rating it'd be 1.75 stars for I've-Read-WAY-Better-But-It's-Not-The-Absolute-Worst.</span>
  <a data-text-id="4817908887381943184" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer1972846383835955729">June 2011:<br/>Just re-read this over the weekend. Still so good. I can't even comprehend how you write an ending like this. Absolutely one of my favorite fictional characters. <br/><br/><br/>April 2008:<br/>I'm not even done with it yet, but stop what you're doing and start reading this book. <br/><br/>From an email to Sarah:<br/><br/>I am reading The Disreputable History of Frankie Landau-Banks.   <span id="freeText1972846383835955729" style="display:none">June 2011:<br />Just re-read this over the weekend. Still so good. I can't even comprehend how you write an ending like this. Absolutely one of my favorite fictional characters. <br /><br /><br />April 2008:<br />I'm not even done with it yet, but stop what you're doing and start reading this book. <br /><br />From an email to Sarah:<br /><br />I am reading The Disreputable History of Frankie Landau-Banks. If the 2nd half is 1/3 as good as the first half has been, it'll be one of favorite books of all time. That's math right there. It's SO GOOD. It's about the battle between good and evil in a teenage girl's soul. I.e. the warring factions of "OMG that boy is so cute I can't believe he wants to kiss me" and "Wait, he wants me to wear his shirt. What does that mean? Is he marking his territory? Am I his doll? Or is he just being nice?" <br /><br />It's all about the societal structures and roles that boys and girls participate in, both willfully and blindly, and how it keeps teenage girls down. EXCEPT ONE.<br /><br />It's so good it's so good it's so good.<br /><br />--<br /><br />All I have to add upon finishing it is that IT'S SO GOOD. I was wondering if was going to just continue along being pleasant and amazing and breezy, but no, at the end it got STRESSFUL, and then I got slightly TEARY, and then it got PERFECT.<br /><br />Klara said that the last page is really good, and she was 100% correct. It's really good.</span>

    <div class="section">
      

<div id="review_1059924875" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1059924875" />
    <a title="Summer" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/14778159-summer"><img alt="Summer" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1574406694p2/14778159.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1059924875?book_show_action=true">Sep 20, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Summer" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Summer" href="/user/show/14778159-summer">Summer</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="did not like it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">did not like it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>


          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">definitely not for people who want to become feminists</span></div>

        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/14778159-summer?shelf=contemporary">contemporary</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1059924875" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8526751167720337019">It's a feminist novel, they said. It won a Printz-award, they said.<br/><br/>What I say:<br/><div>
  <br/>
  <a href='http://1.bp.blogspot.com/-4RlqivNrpgI/VB-yXotjSJI/AAAAAAAABN4/z5y_pLm5XRw/s1600/IMG-20140904-WA0000.jpg' rel='nofollow noopener'>
    <img src='https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1411363508i/11218611.jpg' height='236' width='320' class='gr-hostedUserImg'/>
  </a>
</div><br/>***<br/><br/>The synopsis should have set off sirens in my head.<br/><br/>From the summary alone, what does this seem like? The story of a Mary Sue obsessed with herself. Oh, goody. *eye roll*<br/><br/>In reality, this was a book about a girl who thinks she's better than the entire female population and spends the majority of her high school career trying to prove herself to guys.<br/><br/>Now tell me, how is this in any way "feminist" (which </span>
  <span id="freeText8526751167720337019" style="display:none">It's a feminist novel, they said. It won a Printz-award, they said.<br><br>What I say:<br><div>
  <br>
  <a href="http://1.bp.blogspot.com/-4RlqivNrpgI/VB-yXotjSJI/AAAAAAAABN4/z5y_pLm5XRw/s1600/IMG-20140904-WA0000.jpg" rel="nofollow noopener">
    <img src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/hostedimages/1411363508i/11218611.jpg" height="236" width="320" class="gr-hostedUserImg">
  </a>
</div><br>***<br><br>The synopsis should have set off sirens in my head.<br><br>From the summary alone, what does this seem like? The story of a Mary Sue obsessed with herself. Oh, goody. *eye roll*<br><br>In reality, this was a book about a girl who thinks she's better than the entire female population and spends the majority of her high school career trying to prove herself to guys.<br><br>Now tell me, how is this in any way "feminist" (which the books sets out to be) if all it does is make girls seem lesser than guys? If you ever wanted to read a book that tries extremely hard to be feminist but ends coming off as propaganda, then here it is in the form of a contemporary novel.<br><br>I <b>HATE </b>being preached to.<br><br>And this book does exactly that.<br><br>Say goodbye to subtlety, because TDHoFLB (what an ugly acronym) employs any and all methods possible to shove its personal agenda across--that is, to portray the "ideal female." Forget girl friends, because Frankie is OBVIOUSLY smarter and more intelligent than them. Liking "girly" stuff? Nope, nope, nope. Frankie wants to be the best, and the best is gaining the approval of teenage guys.<br><br>Not only does she crave their validation, but she also paints them as evul beings and the scum of the Earth (how this makes sense, I have no idea). So if all girls are stupid, and all guys are power-hungry, then what is she?<br><br><i>Speshul. Different. A genius.</i> Of course.<br><br>Sigh.<br><br>She rants on and on and tries to prove time and time again how "independent" she is, but why does she pay so much attention to guys if she don't need no men? In the end she comes off as a bratty teenage girl who is used to getting everything she wants, and when she doesn't all hell breaks loose.<br><br>Hey, I'm all for female empowerment and standing up for yourself, but when you get mad over every little minuscule thing that is extremely insignificant in the grand scheme of things, how am I supposed to support you, much less respect you?<br><br>What really made me want to rip my hair out was the part where Frankie refuses to join the field hockey team. Fine, good for you, whatever. But the reason she adamantly rejected it was because <b>
  <u>only girls play the sport.</u>
</b> Are you freaking shitting me? There are no words. RAGE.<br><br><b>BECAUSE IF GUYS DON'T DO IT THEN IT'S <i>OBVIOUSLY </i>NOTHING THAT MATTERS.</b><br><br>
  
    <br>
    
      
        <a href="http://3.bp.blogspot.com/-uZkWpo43BL8/VFWIT-zrGeI/AAAAAAAABQU/RObTLYnt5cE/s1600/467223.gif" rel="nofollow noopener">
          <img src="https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1414892544ra/11701973.gif" class="gr-hostedUserImg">
        </a>
      
    
    <br>
    
      Who says you can't be cute and deadly? *snickers*
    
    <br>
  
<br>Not only was Frankie insanely sexist, but she was also pretentious and snobby. Really, I shouldn't have expected anything less from a book about a high-profile private school with a bunch of privileged white kids and their white kid problems. Go figure.<br><br>There was absolutely nothing positive about this book, aside from the fact that it has a shiny sticker award that proves that it makes kids smarter. #sarcasm Oh, and I learned some new words not-so-subtly interwoven in the writing, straight from an SAT book. Yay for thesauruses!<br><br>I highly doubt I'll be reading any of Lockhart's books at any point in the future. This book just angered me so much that I doubt I'll be able to handle any of her other novels. Moreover, her writing is glaringly pretentious in a way that comes off as <i>I'm-smart-and-clever-and-you're-not-hahaha</i>. AKA, John Green was most likely inspired by you.<br><br>I don't know why everyone and their mom praises this book. <b>I truly don't think this book is the best one to be shoving into teens' hands, nor is it the best portrayal of feminism in YA.</b> In fact, it's probably the worst.<br><br></span>
  <a data-text-id="8526751167720337019" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_84670435" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/84670435" />
    <a title="Minli" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/981514-minli"><img alt="Minli" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1319807416p2/981514.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/84670435?book_show_action=true">Jan 12, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Minli" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Minli" href="/user/show/981514-minli">Minli</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/981514-minli?shelf=contemporary">contemporary</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/981514-minli?shelf=favourites">favourites</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/981514-minli?shelf=young-adult">young-adult</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/981514-minli?shelf=sucky-endings">sucky-endings</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/981514-minli?shelf=book-soulmate">book-soulmate</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/981514-minli?shelf=adorable">adorable</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/981514-minli?shelf=awesome-writing">awesome-writing</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer84670435" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12505600767156742259">5 Things I liked about <i>The Disreputable History of Frankie Landau-Banks</i> by E. Lockhart<br/><br/>1. The female protagonist does NOT spend it lamenting her appearance or lack of popularity/finesse. Nobody likes a sue, but some people actually are curvy, good-looking, confident, funny and smart--though of course they all have insecurities from time to time. But woo-hoo for being well-adjusted! Though we get a lot of Frankie's internal dialogue, she did not strike me as whiny. One of the best things I like ab</span>
  <span id="freeText12505600767156742259" style="display:none">5 Things I liked about <i>The Disreputable History of Frankie Landau-Banks</i> by E. Lockhart<br /><br />1. The female protagonist does NOT spend it lamenting her appearance or lack of popularity/finesse. Nobody likes a sue, but some people actually are curvy, good-looking, confident, funny and smart--though of course they all have insecurities from time to time. But woo-hoo for being well-adjusted! Though we get a lot of Frankie's internal dialogue, she did not strike me as whiny. One of the best things I like about this book is that the protagonist is RATIONAL. She analyzes. She thinks! She debates! <br /><br />2. Lockhart writes 'old school prep' in a way that's realistic (at least, as realistic as I can imagine, myself being on the fringes of that sort of society) and that doesn't make my eyes roll. Frankie seems very conscious of the prestige, wealth, networking and tradition that goes with a stuffy school like Alabaster. Popularity and entitlement are not wish-fulfilling fantasies, but essential elements that make up her world.<br /><br />3. Frankie is by no means a perfect character. In fact, the flaws in the main characters are what makes the novel so fantastic: Frankie herself, Matthew, and Alpha, and to some lesser extent Elizabeth and Porter. My favourite supporting characters were probably Trish (the cookie baking feminist who is not ashamed of liking goofy guys who treat her nice and staying away from loud drunken parties) and Zada (Frankie's sister in her first year of college, who decided to go to Berkeley instead of Harvard, breaking their father's heart).<br /><br />4. The story is set at a boarding school, with emphasis on <i>school</i>. There are lessons involved. Frankie learns things in classes that are later important to the plot. The schoolwork is interesting! I liked the musings on grammar, sociology/behaviour, surveillance and feminist studies. <br /><br />5. The judicious use of the emails, notes, school papers and handwritten letters in the text. Some of the emails are laugh-out-loud funny, and the ones that aren't are interesting. <br /><br />A fantastic read--if I liked to write, it would be the kind of book I wish I'd written. The only quibble I have with it is the ending, and that's just out of personal preference--I would have liked it to end on a more optimistic note, like perhaps at a Geek Conglomerate meeting where Frankie realises that there is more to Alabaster than the prestige/brotherhood of The Loyal Order of the Basset Hounds. And she knows it in her heart, but that scene with Matthew so many weeks later with Frankie still hurt over his cold rejection, just makes me shake. She's brilliant, and the book would have been more satisfying to me if the empowering part of the coda was longer. </span>
  <a data-text-id="12505600767156742259" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_30788997" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/30788997" />
    <a title="Sonia Reppe" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/455200-sonia-reppe"><img alt="Sonia Reppe" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1454770486p2/455200.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/30788997?book_show_action=true">Aug 21, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Sonia Reppe" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Sonia Reppe" href="/user/show/455200-sonia-reppe">Sonia Reppe</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/455200-sonia-reppe?shelf=young-adult-teen-lit">young-adult-teen-lit</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/455200-sonia-reppe?shelf=boarding-school">boarding-school</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer30788997" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2547204316847921963">First of all, I agree with feminism and equal opportunities and all that, and I appreciate that this book had a "girl power" theme, but the main character was not a good representation of an admirable woman. She was intelligent and competative, but was also the worst stereotype of a jealous, conniving, sneaky bitch. I thought she made girls look totally bad. She was jealous that her boyfriend was in a secret all-male social club, and was somehow insulted that she wasn't invited and that he would</span>
  <span id="freeText2547204316847921963" style="display:none">First of all, I agree with feminism and equal opportunities and all that, and I appreciate that this book had a "girl power" theme, but the main character was not a good representation of an admirable woman. She was intelligent and competative, but was also the worst stereotype of a jealous, conniving, sneaky bitch. I thought she made girls look totally bad. She was jealous that her boyfriend was in a secret all-male social club, and was somehow insulted that she wasn't invited and that he wouldn't tell her about it. Frankie, it's a secret boys club! Start your own secret girls club! That's my opinion. She was so annoying. If a character said something like, for example, "boys are more agressive than girls because of their testosterone"—which is true, right? (I personally believe there wouldn't be any wars if there weren't any men) she jumped all over them with a smart rebuttal. Her boyfriend couldn't even call her "adorable" or give her his shirt to wear without arising the debate of: is he marking his territory or does he respect her for her mind? These are good questions to ask, but the problem I have is, I did not buy that Frankie was in love with Matthew at all, even though the book said she was. The book also did not acknowledge any such thing as chemistry between them. Frankie was all sore that Matthew's buddies had higher status than she did with him; how DARE he put them before HER when she has been going out with him for THREE WHOLE MONTHS! OMG! Maybe another reason I didn't connect with Frankie was the brisk tone of the book. Her story was chronicled with a hip, journalistic brevity. My last thoughts: guys love to do guy things; what's wrong with that? They will play video games and watch sports till the end of time, and ignore their women while doing it. Frankie, ironically, seemed so needy. I thought Frankie went about making her point in the wrong way (she basically broke a lot of rules) and ultimately, it was about getting the attention of the boys. </span>
  <a data-text-id="2547204316847921963" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1064930059" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3070540-kristina-horner?shelf=favorites">favorites</a>,

          </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1064930059" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11435125951372479033">Gah, I loved this book so incredibly much, but upon finishing I find myself so RILED UP and ANGRY on Frankie's behalf and I understand her frustrations so fully. The feminist themes in this story were executed with grace and keen wit, and it never felt preachy. I just wanted to reach in the book and hug Frankie and let her know that some day, people on Tumblr would totally understand what she's going through. Her struggles were so real and I just enjoyed it so immensely. Highly recommend. </span>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1126989906" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1126989906" />
    <a title="Melanie" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/10890208-melanie"><img alt="Melanie" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1413275441p2/10890208.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1126989906?book_show_action=true">Dec 08, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Melanie" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Melanie" href="/user/show/10890208-melanie">Melanie</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781760113308" href="/book/show/23720331-the-disreputable-history-of-frankie-landau-banks">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/10890208-melanie?shelf=could-have-been-so-much-better">could-have-been-so-much-better</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/10890208-melanie?shelf=bleh">bleh</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/10890208-melanie?shelf=romance">romance</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/10890208-melanie?shelf=arc">arc</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/10890208-melanie?shelf=young-adult">young-adult</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/10890208-melanie?shelf=books-i-own">books-i-own</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/10890208-melanie?shelf=contemporary">contemporary</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1126989906" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3847802959338662678">See more reviews at <a href='http://yamidnightreads.com/' rel='nofollow noopener'>YA Midnight Reads</a><br/><br/><strong>Reading <em>The Disreputable History of Frankie Landau-Banks </em>just goes to show that loving one book by an author doesn't guarantee you'll love every book by that said author.</strong> (I should know this after reading so many books but no, I am a person who never learns ._.) I read <em>We Were Liars </em>almost a year ago and well, let's just say it made my top 10 reads of 2014. (If you want to know why I loved it, I suggest you check out <a href='http://yamidnightreads.com/2014/07/discussion-review-we-were-liars-by-e-lockhart/' target='_blank' rel='nofollow noopener'>the discussion review/fangirling session</a> C</span>
  <span id="freeText3847802959338662678" style="display:none">See more reviews at <a href="http://yamidnightreads.com/" rel="nofollow noopener">YA Midnight Reads</a><br><br><strong>Reading <em>The Disreputable History of Frankie Landau-Banks </em>just goes to show that loving one book by an author doesn't guarantee you'll love every book by that said author.</strong> (I should know this after reading so many books but no, I am a person who never learns ._.) I read <em>We Were Liars </em>almost a year ago and well, let's just say it made my top 10 reads of 2014. (If you want to know why I loved it, I suggest you check out <a href="http://yamidnightreads.com/2014/07/discussion-review-we-were-liars-by-e-lockhart/" target="_blank" rel="nofollow noopener">the discussion review/fangirling session</a> Celine and I did.) Unfortunately for this book, there's no doubt it won't be landing my top 10 for 2015.<br><blockquote>It is better to be alone, she figures, than to be with someone who can’t see who you are. It is better to lead than to follow. It is better to speak up than stay silent. It is better to open doors than to shut them on people.</blockquote><br>So apparently, this is a feminist book. I cannot remember who told me that it was, but I certainly went into this thinking it was going to be a pro-feminist read.<strong> Yet nope, I didn't see <em>The Disreputable History of Frankie Landau-Banks </em>as a feminist novel at all.</strong> We are introduced to Frankie as a strong-headed and determined girl who doesn't like being talked to condescendingly and hates taking 'no' for an answer. I wanted to love this girl, for she initially sounded like someone I can strongly identify with, but I soon was proved wrong--<strong>Frankie got less likeable more and more throughout the book</strong>. Here are the reasons why I was completely ticked off by her.<br><br>Frankie gets pretty irked when she discovers that there is a secretive all boys society which is known as the Basset Hounds. Her boyfriend, Matthew Livingstone, is apart of it too and what pisses her of more is that Matthew did not tell her about this society. Soon Frankie decides that she wants in on this all males society which, to me, sounded completely absurd in the first place. I mean really, the things that the boys do in this society is so freakin' lame. Anyways, Frankie soon starts stalking her boyfriend and decides that the only way she'll get into the Basset Hounds is to prove to the boys that she's way better than all of them. Which, didn't make any sense to me. If Frankie felt that she was better than the boys, why even bother dealing with in the first place?--they're not worth her time and efforts. And this leads me to my second complaint about Frankie. <strong>She was constantly trying to prove herself to Matthew and the other boys.</strong> When one of the boys said something to her (mainly Matthew), Frankie would analyse and go through all the possible responses then select the best one to reply with for the Matthew and the boys to respect her more. <strong>Essentially, this book was about Frankie who followed the boys around everywhere just so she could get their approval of her. Does that sound like a feminist novel to you?</strong><br><br>No, I didn't think so.<br><br>And don't let me get started on how much the boys in this novel annoyed me. <strong>These boys don't recognise (or admit to recognise) a girl until she starts getting fat in the right places.</strong> HOW RIDICULOUS IS THAT?<br><br><img src="https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1412815477ra/11427795.gif" alt="" width="400" class="gr-hostedUserImg"><br><br><strong>What I did appreciate about this novel was Frankie's wit.</strong> She's a really smart girl, who just didn't put her efforts in the right places. I particularly liked Frankie's 'neglected positive' words and it was really entertaining to read about. <strong>Sure, her motives and ideas totally sucked, but there were a few positives about Frankie, and I'm not ready to just forget them despite how much I hated the plot line. </strong><br><br>In my opinion, E. Lockhart<strong> tried to achieve too much</strong> here. This is certainly <strong>not a feminist novel</strong> and so many characters here put me on edge and how obviously rude they were (namely, the boys--like Matthew Livingstone). <strong>I won't be recommending this book, so if you want to try E. Lockhart, I suggest you turn to a much better novel by her; <em>We Were Liars</em>. </strong><br><p>
  <i>~Thank you Allen and Unwin Australia for sending me this copy~</i>
</p><br><br>---<br><br><img src="https://images.gr-assets.com/hostedimages/1408590024ra/10870307.gif" width="400" alt=" " class="gr-hostedUserImg"></span>
